infrastructure. These are provided more or less routinely, in a more or less equal fashion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JonesEtAl1980_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-JonesEtAl1980-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lineberry_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lineberry-114">[113]</a></sup> Responsibility for administration usually falls on the city government, though some services may be operated by a higher level of government,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> while others may be privately run.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup> Armies may assume responsibility for policing cities in states of domestic turmoil such as America\'s <a href="/wiki/King_assassination_riots" title="King assassination riots">King assassination riots</a> of 1968.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finance">Finance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Finance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The traditional basis for municipal finance is local <a href="/wiki/Property_tax" title="Property tax">property tax</a> levied on <a href="/wiki/Real_estate" title="Real estate">real estate</a> within the city. Local government can also collect revenue for services, or by leasing land that it owns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gwilliam2013_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gwilliam2013-117">[116]</a></sup> However, financing municipal services, as well as <a href="/wiki/Urban_renewal" title="Urban renewal">urban renewal</a> and other development projects, is a perennial problem, which cities address through appeals to higher governments, arrangements with the private sector, and techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Privatization" title="Privatization">privatization</a> (selling services into the <a href="/wiki/Private_sector" title="Private sector">private sector</a>), <a href="/wiki/Corporatization" title="Corporatization">corporatization</a> (formation of quasi-private municipally-owned corporations), and <a href="/wiki/Financialization" title="Financialization">financialization</a> (packaging city assets into tradable <a href="/wiki/Financial_instrument" title="Financial instrument">financial instruments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Derivative_(finance)" title="Derivative (finance)">derivatives</a>). This situation has become acute in deindustrialized cities and in cases where businesses and wealthier citizens have moved outside of <a href="/wiki/City_limits" title="City limits">city limits</a> and therefore beyond the reach of taxation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weber2010_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weber2010-121">[120]</a></sup> Cities in search of <a href="/wiki/Cash_and_cash_equivalents" title="Cash and cash equivalents">ready cash</a> increasingly resort to the <a href="/wiki/Municipal_bond" title="Municipal bond">municipal bond</a>, essentially a loan with <a href="/wiki/Maturity_(finance)" title="Maturity (finance)">interest</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Maturity_(finance)" title="Maturity (finance)">repayment date</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> City governments have also begun to use <a href="/wiki/Tax_increment_financing" title="Tax increment financing">tax increment financing</a>, in which a development project is financed by loans based on future tax revenues which it is expected to yield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weber2010_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weber2010-121">[120]</a></sup> Under these circumstances, creditors and consequently city governments place a high importance on city <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating" title="Credit rating">credit ratings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Governance" title="Governance">Governance</a> includes government but refers to a wider domain of <a href="/wiki/Social_control" title="Social control">social control</a> functions implemented by many actors including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nongovernmental_organization" title="Nongovernmental organization">nongovernmental organizations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> The impact of globalization and the role of <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational corporations</a> in local governments worldwide, has led to a shift in perspective on urban governance, away from the "urban regime theory" in which a coalition of local interests functionally govern, toward a theory of outside economic control, widely associated in academics with the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Neoliberalism" title="Neoliberalism">neoliberalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> In the neoliberal model of governance, public utilities are <a href="/wiki/Privatization" title="Privatization">privatized</a>, industry is <a href="/wiki/Deregulation" title="Deregulation">deregulated</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Corporation" title="Corporation">corporations</a> gain the status of governing actors&#8212;as indicated by the power they wield in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public-private_partnerships" title="Public-private partnerships">public-private partnerships</a> and over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Business_improvement_districts" title="Business improvement districts">business improvement districts</a>, and in the expectation of self-regulation through <a href="/wiki/Corporate_social_responsibility" title="Corporate social responsibility">corporate social responsibility</a>. The biggest <a href="/wiki/Investor" title="Investor">investors</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_estate_developer" title="Real estate developer">real estate developers</a> act as the city\'s <a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a> urban planners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The related concept of <a href="/wiki/Good_governance" title="Good governance">good governance</a> places more emphasis on the state, with the purpose of assessing urban governments for their suitability for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Development_assistance" title="Development assistance">development assistance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta2015p33_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta2015p33-127">[126]</a></sup> The concepts of governance and good governance are especially invoked in the emergent megacities, where international organizations consider existing governments inadequate for their large populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_planning">Urban planning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Urban planning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_Plata_desde_el_aire.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/La_Plata_desde_el_aire.JPG/300px-La_Plata_desde_el_aire.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/La_Plata_desde_el_aire.JPG/450px-La_Plata_desde_el_aire.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/La_Plata_desde_el_aire.JPG/600px-La_Plata_desde_el_aire.JPG 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_Plata_desde_el_aire.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/La_Plata" title="La Plata">La Plata</a>, Argentina, based on a perfect square with 5196-meter sides, was designed in the 1880s as the new capital of <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires_Province" title="Buenos Aires Province">Buenos Aires Province</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">Urban planning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urban_design" title="Urban design">Urban design</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">Urban planning</a>, the application of forethought to city design, involves optimizing land use, transportation, utilities, and other basic systems, in order to achieve <a href="/wiki/Technical_aspects_of_urban_planning" title="Technical aspects of urban planning">certain objectives</a>. Urban planners and scholars have proposed overlapping <a href="/wiki/Theories_of_urban_planning" title="Theories of urban planning">theories</a> as ideals for how plans should be formed. Planning tools, beyond the original design of the city itself, include <a href="/wiki/Public_capital" title="Public capital">public capital</a> investment in infrastructure and <a href="/wiki/Land-use_planning" title="Land-use planning">land-use controls</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Zoning" title="Zoning">zoning</a>. The continuous process of <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_planning" title="Comprehensive planning">comprehensive planning</a> involves identifying general objectives as well as collecting data to evaluate progress and inform future decisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McQuillin1987planning_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-McQuillin1987planning-131">[130]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Government, as the <a href="/wiki/Monopoly_on_violence" title="Monopoly on violence">ultimate wielder of force</a> is legally the final authority on planning but in practice the process involves both public and private elements. The legal principle of <a href="/wiki/Eminent_domain" title="Eminent domain">eminent domain</a> is used by government to divest citizens of their property in cases where its use is required for a project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McQuillin1987planning_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-McQuillin1987planning-131">[130]</a></sup> Planning often involves tradeoffs&#8212;decisions in which some stand to gain and some to lose&#8212;and thus is closely connected to the prevailing political situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_urban_planning" title="History of urban planning">history of urban planning</a> dates to some of the earliest known cities, especially in the Indus Valley and Mesoamerican civilizations, which built their cities on grids and apparently zoned different areas for different purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2002_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2002-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup> The effects of planning, ubiquitous in today\'s world, can be seen most clearly in the layout of <a href="/wiki/Planned_community" title="Planned community">planned communities</a>, fully designed prior to construction, often with consideration for interlocking physical, economic, and cultural systems.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_structure">Social structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Social structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Urban_sociology" title="Urban sociology">Urban society</a> is typically <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">stratified</a>. Spatially, cities are formally or informally <a href="/wiki/Geographical_segregation" title="Geographical segregation">segregated</a> along ethnic, economic and racial lines. People living relatively close together may live, work, and play, in separate areas, and associate with different people, forming <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_enclave" title="Ethnic enclave">ethnic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lifestyle_enclave" title="Lifestyle enclave">lifestyle</a> enclaves or, in areas of concentrated poverty, <a href="/wiki/Ghetto" title="Ghetto">ghettoes</a>. While in the US and elsewhere poverty became associated with the <a href="/wiki/Inner_city" title="Inner city">inner city</a>, in France it has become associated with the <i><a href="/wiki/Banlieue" title="Banlieue">banlieues</a></i>, areas of urban development which surround the city proper. Meanwhile, across Europe and North America, the racially <a href="/wiki/White_people" title="White people">white</a> majority is empirically the most segregated group. <a href="/wiki/Suburb" title="Suburb">Suburbs</a> in the west, and, increasingly, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gated_communities" title="Gated communities">gated communities</a> and other forms of "privatopia" around the world, allow local elites to self-segregate into secure and exclusive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neighborhood" title="Neighborhood">neighborhoods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Landless urban workers, contrasted with <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasants</a> and known as the <a href="/wiki/Proletariat" title="Proletariat">proletariat</a>, form a growing stratum of society in the age of urbanization. In <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxist</a> doctrine, the proletariat will inevitably <a href="/wiki/Proletarian_revolution" title="Proletarian revolution">revolt</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeoisie</a> as their ranks swell with disenfranchised and disaffected people lacking all stake in the <a href="/wiki/Status_quo" title="Status quo">status quo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup> The global urban proletariat of today, however, generally lacks the status as factory workers which in the nineteenth century provided access to the <a href="/wiki/Means_of_production" title="Means of production">means of production</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis2004_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davis2004-136">[135]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Historically, cities rely on <a href="/wiki/Rural_area" title="Rural area">rural areas</a> for <a href="/wiki/Intensive_farming" title="Intensive farming">intensive farming</a> to <a href="/wiki/Crop_yield" title="Crop yield">yield surplus crops</a>, in exchange for which they provide money, political administration, manufactured goods, and culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan2004p155_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan2004p155-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall1989p15_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall1989p15-28">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Urban_economics" title="Urban economics">Urban economics</a> tends to analyze larger agglomerations, stretching beyond city limits, in order to reach a more complete understanding of the local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labor_market" title="Labor market">labor market</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Market_in_Taipei_-_DSC01062.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="5472" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Market_in_Taipei_-_DSC01062.JPG/220px-Market_in_Taipei_-_DSC01062.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Market_in_Taipei_-_DSC01062.JPG/330px-Market_in_Taipei_-_DSC01062.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Market_in_Taipei_-_DSC01062.JPG/440px-Market_in_Taipei_-_DSC01062.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Market_in_Taipei_-_DSC01062.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>People shopping for <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a> at a marketplace in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taipei_City" title="Taipei City">Taipei City</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>As hubs of trade cities have long been home to <a href="/wiki/Retail" title="Retail">retail</a> commerce and <a href="/wiki/Consumption_(economics)" title="Consumption (economics)">consumption</a> through the interface of <a href="/wiki/Shopping" title="Shopping">shopping</a>. In the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Department_store" title="Department store">department stores</a> using new techniques of <a href="/wiki/Advertising" title="Advertising">advertising</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_relations" title="Public relations">public relations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decorative_arts" title="Decorative arts">decoration</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Design" title="Design">design</a>, transformed urban shopping areas into <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_world" title="Fantasy world">fantasy worlds</a> encouraging self-expression and escape through <a href="/wiki/Consumerism" title="Consumerism">consumerism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In general, the density of cities expedites commerce and facilitates <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_spillover" title="Knowledge spillover">knowledge spillovers</a>, helping people and firms exchange information and generate new ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CalderFreytas2009_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-CalderFreytas2009-141">[140]</a></sup> A thicker labor market allows for better skill matching between firms and individuals. Population density enables also sharing of common infrastructure and production facilities, however in very dense cities, increased crowding and waiting times may lead to some negative effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">manufacturing</a> fueled the growth of cities, many now rely on a <a href="/wiki/Tertiary_sector_of_the_economy" title="Tertiary sector of the economy">tertiary</a> or <a href="/wiki/Service_economy" title="Service economy">service economy</a>. The services in question range from <a href="/wiki/Tourism" title="Tourism">tourism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hospitality_industry" title="Hospitality industry">hospitality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Entertainment" title="Entertainment">entertainment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Housekeeping" title="Housekeeping">housekeeping</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prostitution" title="Prostitution">prostitution</a> to <a href="/wiki/Grey-collar" title="Grey-collar">grey-collar</a> work in <a href="/wiki/Legal_outsourcing" title="Legal outsourcing">law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Financial_services" title="Financial services">finance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Management" title="Management">administration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan2004p164_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan2004p164-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture_and_communications">Culture and communications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Culture and communications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Cities are typically hubs for <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_arts" title="The arts">the arts</a>, supporting <a href="/wiki/University" title="University">universities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Museum" title="Museum">museums</a>, <a href="/wiki/Temple" title="Temple">temples</a>, and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_institutions" title="Cultural institutions">cultural institutions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall14_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall14-19">[19]</a></sup> They feature impressive displays of <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a> ranging from small to enormous and ornate to <a href="/wiki/Brutalist_architecture" title="Brutalist architecture">brutal</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skyscrapers" title="Skyscrapers">skyscrapers</a>, providing thousands of offices or homes within a small footprint, and visible from miles away, have become iconic urban features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup> Cultural elites tend to live in cities, bound together by shared <a href="/wiki/Cultural_capital" title="Cultural capital">cultural capital</a>, and themselves playing some role in governance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup> By virtue of their status as centers of culture and literacy, cities can be described as the locus of <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_world" title="History of the world">world history</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Social_change" title="Social change">social change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Density makes for effective <a href="/wiki/Mass_communication" title="Mass communication">mass communication</a> and transmission of <a href="/wiki/News" title="News">news</a>, through <a href="/wiki/Herald" title="Herald">heralds</a>, printed <a href="/wiki/Proclamation" title="Proclamation">proclamations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newspaper" title="Newspaper">newspapers</a>, and digital media. These communication networks, though still using cities as hubs, penetrate extensively into all populated areas. In the age of rapid communication and transportation, commentators have described urban culture as nearly ubiquitous<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HugoEtAl2003_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-HugoEtAl2003-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup> or as no longer meaningful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Today, a city\'s promotion of its cultural activities dovetails with <a href="/wiki/Place_branding" title="Place branding">place branding</a> and <a href="/wiki/City_marketing" title="City marketing">city marketing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_diplomacy" title="Public diplomacy">public diplomacy</a> techniques used to inform development strategy; to attract businesses, investors, residents, and tourists; and to create a <a href="/wiki/Collective_identity" title="Collective identity">shared identity</a>  and <a href="/wiki/Sense_of_place" title="Sense of place">sense of place</a> within the metropolitan area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wachsmuth2014_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wachsmuth2014-152">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KerrOliver2015_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-KerrOliver2015-154">[153]</a></sup> Physical inscriptions, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historical_marker" title="Historical marker">plaques,</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monument" title="Monument">monuments</a> on display physically transmit a historical context for urban places.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup> Some cities, such as <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> have indelible religious status and for hundreds of years have attracted <a href="/wiki/Pilgrim" title="Pilgrim">pilgrims</a>. Patriotic tourists visit <a href="/wiki/Agra" title="Agra">Agra</a> to see the <a href="/wiki/Taj_Mahal" title="Taj Mahal">Taj Mahal</a>, or <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> to visit the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="World Trade Center (2001&#8211;present)">World Trade Center</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elvis" title="Elvis">Elvis</a> lovers visit <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis</a> to pay their respects at <a href="/wiki/Graceland" title="Graceland">Graceland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup> Place brands (which include place satisfaction and place loyalty) have great economic value (comparable to the value of commodity <a href="/wiki/Brand" title="Brand">brands</a>) because of their influence on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decision-making_process" title="Decision-making process">decision-making process</a> of people thinking about doing business in&#8212;"purchasing" (the brand of)&#8212;a city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KerrOliver2015_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-KerrOliver2015-154">[153]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bread_and_circuses" title="Bread and circuses">Bread and circuses</a> among other forms of cultural appeal, attract and entertain <a href="/wiki/Commoner" title="Commoner">the masses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoholyNagy1968p136_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-MoholyNagy1968p136-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup> Sports also play a major role in city branding and local <a href="/wiki/Identity_(social_science)" title="Identity (social science)">identity</a> formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup> Cities go to considerable lengths in competing to host the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a>, which bring global attention and tourism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward2008_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward2008-159">[158]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare">Warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Second_World_War_1939-45-_Victory_and_Aftermath_IND5196.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="779" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/The_Second_World_War_1939-45-_Victory_and_Aftermath_IND5196.jpg/220px-The_Second_World_War_1939-45-_Victory_and_Aftermath_IND5196.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/The_Second_World_War_1939-45-_Victory_and_Aftermath_IND5196.jpg/330px-The_Second_World_War_1939-45-_Victory_and_Aftermath_IND5196.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/The_Second_World_War_1939-45-_Victory_and_Aftermath_IND5196.jpg/440px-The_Second_World_War_1939-45-_Victory_and_Aftermath_IND5196.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Second_World_War_1939-45-_Victory_and_Aftermath_IND5196.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">Atomic bombing</a> on August 6, 1945, devastated the Japanese city of <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Cities play a crucial strategic role in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warfare" title="Warfare">warfare</a> due to their economic, demographic, symbolic, and political centrality. For the same reasons, they are targets in <a href="/wiki/Asymmetric_warfare" title="Asymmetric warfare">asymmetric warfare</a>. Many cities throughout history were founded under military auspices, a great many have incorporated <a href="/wiki/Fortification" title="Fortification">fortifications</a>, and military principles continue to <a href="/wiki/Military_urbanism" title="Military urbanism">influence urban design</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> Indeed, war may have served as the social rationale and economic basis for the very earliest cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mumfurd1961war_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mumfurd1961war-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ashworth1991p12_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ashworth1991p12-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Powers engaged in <a href="/wiki/Geopolitics" title="Geopolitics">geopolitical</a> conflict have established fortified settlements as part of military strategies, as in the case of <a href="/wiki/Garrison" title="Garrison">garrison</a> towns, America\'s <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Hamlet_Program" title="Strategic Hamlet Program">Strategic Hamlet Program</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Israeli_settlement" title="Israeli settlement">Israeli settlements</a> in Palestine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup> While <a href="/wiki/Philippine%E2%80%93American_War" title="Philippine&#8211;American War">occupying</a> the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, the US Army ordered local people concentrated into cities and towns, in order to isolate committed insurgents and battle freely against them in the countryside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Destroyed_Warsaw,_capital_of_Poland,_January_1945.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="824" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Destroyed_Warsaw%2C_capital_of_Poland%2C_January_1945.jpg/220px-Destroyed_Warsaw%2C_capital_of_Poland%2C_January_1945.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Destroyed_Warsaw%2C_capital_of_Poland%2C_January_1945.jpg/330px-Destroyed_Warsaw%2C_capital_of_Poland%2C_January_1945.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Destroyed_Warsaw%2C_capital_of_Poland%2C_January_1945.jpg/440px-Destroyed_Warsaw%2C_capital_of_Poland%2C_January_1945.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Destroyed_Warsaw,_capital_of_Poland,_January_1945.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Warsaw Old Town after the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Uprising" title="Warsaw Uprising">Warsaw Uprising</a>, 85% of the city was <a href="/wiki/Aftermath_of_the_Warsaw_Uprising" title="Aftermath of the Warsaw Uprising">deliberately destroyed</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, national governments on occasion declared certain cities <a href="/wiki/Open_city" title="Open city">open</a>, effectively <a href="/wiki/Surrender_(military)" title="Surrender (military)">surrendering</a> them to an advancing enemy in order to avoid damage and bloodshed. Urban warfare proved decisive, however, in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stalingrad" title="Battle of Stalingrad">Battle of Stalingrad</a>, where Soviet forces repulsed German occupiers, with extreme casualties and destruction. In an era of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Low-intensity_conflict&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Low-intensity conflict (page does not exist)">low-intensity conflict</a> and rapid urbanization, cities have become sites of long-term conflict waged both by foreign occupiers and by local governments against <a href="/wiki/Insurgency" title="Insurgency">insurgency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis2004_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davis2004-136">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup> Such warfare, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counterinsurgency" title="Counterinsurgency">counterinsurgency</a>, involves techniques of surveillance and <a href="/wiki/Psychological_warfare" title="Psychological warfare">psychological warfare</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Close_combat" title="Close combat">close combat</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup> functionally extends modern urban <a href="/wiki/Crime_prevention" title="Crime prevention">crime prevention</a>, which already uses concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Defensible_space_theory" title="Defensible space theory">defensible space</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although capture is the more common objective, warfare has in some cases spelt complete destruction for a city. Mesopotamian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuneiform_script#List_of_major_Cuneiform_tablet_discoveries" title="Cuneiform script">tablets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruins" title="Ruins">ruins</a> attest to such destruction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup> as does the Latin motto <i><a href="/wiki/Carthago_delenda_est" title="Carthago delenda est">Carthago delenda est</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup> Since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_bombing_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombing of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">atomic bombing of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a> and throughout the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_strategy" title="Nuclear strategy">nuclear strategists</a> continued to contemplate the use of "<a href="/wiki/Countervalue" title="Countervalue">countervalue</a>" targeting: crippling an enemy by annihilating its valuable cities, rather than <a href="/wiki/Counterforce" title="Counterforce">aiming primarily at its military forces</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">Infrastructure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Infrastructure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Urban <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">infrastructure</a> involves various physical networks and spaces necessary for transportation, water use, energy, recreation, and public functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tarr_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tarr-172">[171]</a></sup> Infrastructure carries a high initial cost in <a href="/wiki/Fixed_capital" title="Fixed capital">fixed capital</a> (pipes, wires, plants, vehicles, etc.) but lower <a href="/wiki/Marginal_cost" title="Marginal cost">marginal costs</a> and thus positive <a href="/wiki/Economies_of_scale" title="Economies of scale">economies of scale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WellmanSpiller2012_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-WellmanSpiller2012-173">[172]</a></sup> Because of the higher <a href="/wiki/Barriers_to_entry" title="Barriers to entry">barriers to entry</a>, these networks have been classified as <a href="/wiki/Natural_monopoly" title="Natural monopoly">natural monopolies</a>, meaning that economic logic favors control of each network by a single organization, public or private.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bakker2003_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bakker2003-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WellmanPretorius2012_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-WellmanPretorius2012-174">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[b]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Infrastructure in general (if not every infrastructure project) plays a vital role in a city\'s capacity for economic activity and expansion, underpinning the very survival of the city\'s inhabitants, as well as technological, commercial, industrial, and social activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tarr_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tarr-172">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WellmanSpiller2012_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-WellmanSpiller2012-173">[172]</a></sup> Structurally, many infrastructure systems take the form of <a href="/wiki/Network_theory" title="Network theory">networks</a> with redundant links and multiple pathways, so that the system as a whole continue to operate even if parts of it fail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WellmanPretorius2012_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-WellmanPretorius2012-174">[173]</a></sup> The particulars of a city\'s infrastructure systems have historical <a href="/wiki/Path_dependence" title="Path dependence">path dependence</a> because new development must build from what exists already.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WellmanSpiller2012_173-2"><a href="#cite_note-WellmanSpiller2012-173">[172]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Megaproject" title="Megaproject">Megaprojects</a> such as the construction of <a href="/wiki/Airport" title="Airport">airports</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_plant" title="Power plant">power plants</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Railway" title="Railway">railways</a> require large upfront investments and thus tend to require funding from national government or the private sector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WellmanPretorius2012_174-2"><a href="#cite_note-WellmanPretorius2012-174">[173]</a></sup> Privatization may also extend to all levels of infrastructure construction and maintenance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[177]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Urban infrastructure ideally serves all residents equally but in practice may prove uneven&#8212;with, in some cities, clear first-class and second-class alternatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lineberry_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lineberry-114">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bakker2003_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bakker2003-106">[105]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Utilities">Utilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Utilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Public_utility" title="Public utility">Public utilities</a> (literally, useful things with general availability) include basic and essential infrastructure networks, chiefly concerned with the supply of water, electricity, and telecommunications capability to the populace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[179]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">Sanitation</a>, necessary for good health in crowded conditions, requires water supply and <a href="/wiki/Waste_management" title="Waste management">waste management</a> as well as individual <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a>. Urban <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> systems include principally a <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_network" title="Water supply network">water supply network</a> and a network for <a href="/wiki/Wastewater" title="Wastewater">wastewater</a> including <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stormwater" title="Stormwater">stormwater</a>. <a href="/wiki/History_of_water_supply_and_sanitation" title="History of water supply and sanitation">Historically</a>, either local governments or private companies have administered urban <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">water supply</a>, with a tendency toward government water supply in the 20th century and a tendency toward private operation at the turn of the twenty-first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bakker2003_106-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bakker2003-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[c]</a></sup> The market for private water services is dominated by two French companies, <a href="/wiki/Veolia_Water" title="Veolia Water">Veolia Water</a> (formerly <a href="/wiki/Vivendi" title="Vivendi">Vivendi</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Engie" title="Engie">Engie</a> (formerly <a href="/wiki/Suez_(company)" title="Suez (company)">Suez</a>), said to hold 70% of all water contracts worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bakker2003_106-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bakker2003-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[181]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Modern urban life relies heavily on the <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> transmitted through <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> for the operation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_machines" title="Electric machines">electric machines</a> (from household <a href="/wiki/Home_appliance" title="Home appliance">appliances</a> to <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_industrial_machinery" title="Outline of industrial machinery">industrial machines</a> to now-ubiquitous <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronic</a> systems used in communications, business, and government) and for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traffic_lights" title="Traffic lights">traffic lights</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Streetlights" title="Streetlights">streetlights</a> and indoor <a href="/wiki/Lighting" title="Lighting">lighting</a>. Cities rely to a lesser extent on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon_fuel" title="Hydrocarbon fuel">hydrocarbon fuels</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> for transportation, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heating" title="Heating">heating</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cooking" title="Cooking">cooking</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telecommunications" title="Telecommunications">Telecommunications</a> infrastructure such as <a href="/wiki/Telephone_line" title="Telephone line">telephone lines</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coaxial_cables" title="Coaxial cables">coaxial cables</a> also traverse cities, forming dense networks for <a href="/wiki/Mass_communication" title="Mass communication">mass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Point-to-point_(telecommunications)" title="Point-to-point (telecommunications)">point-to-point</a> communications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[182]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Public_transport" title="Public transport">Public transport</a></div>\n<p>Because cities rely on specialization and an <a href="/wiki/Economic_system" title="Economic system">economic system</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Wage_labour" title="Wage labour">wage labour</a>, their inhabitants must have the ability to regularly travel between home, work, commerce, and entertainment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[183]</a></sup> Citydwellers travel foot or by wheel on <a href="/wiki/Road" title="Road">roads</a> and <a href="/wiki/Walkway" title="Walkway">walkways</a>, or use special <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit" title="Rapid transit">rapid transit</a> systems based on <a href="/wiki/Tunnel" title="Tunnel">underground</a>, <a href="/wiki/Light_rail" title="Light rail">overground</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Elevated_railway" title="Elevated railway">elevated</a> rail. Cities also rely on long-distance transportation (truck, <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport" title="Rail transport">rail</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Airplane" title="Airplane">airplane</a>) for economic connections with other cities and rural areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hart2001_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hart2001-187">[184]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:81-540.2K-541.2K_Dnipro_station.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="647" data-file-width="984" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/81-540.2K-541.2K_Dnipro_station.jpg/220px-81-540.2K-541.2K_Dnipro_station.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/81-540.2K-541.2K_Dnipro_station.jpg/330px-81-540.2K-541.2K_Dnipro_station.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/81-540.2K-541.2K_Dnipro_station.jpg/440px-81-540.2K-541.2K_Dnipro_station.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:81-540.2K-541.2K_Dnipro_station.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Train" title="Train">Train</a> stopped at the <a href="/wiki/Dnipro_(Kiev_Metro)" title="Dnipro (Kiev Metro)">Dnipro stop</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Kiev_Metro" title="Kiev Metro">Kiev Metro</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Historically, city streets were the domain of <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">horses</a> and their riders and <a href="/wiki/Pedestrian" title="Pedestrian">pedestrians</a>, who only sometimes had <a href="/wiki/Sidewalk" title="Sidewalk">sidewalks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transit_mall" title="Transit mall">special walking areas</a> reserved for them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[185]</a></sup> In the west, <a href="/wiki/Bicycle" title="Bicycle">bicycles</a> or (<a href="/wiki/Velocipede" title="Velocipede">velocipedes</a>), efficient human-powered machines for short- and medium-distance travel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[186]</a></sup> enjoyed a period of popularity at the beginning of the twentieth century before the rise of automobiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[187]</a></sup> Soon after, they gained a more lasting foothold in Asian and African cities under European influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[188]</a></sup> In western cities, industrializing, expanding, and <a href="/wiki/Electrification" title="Electrification">electrifying</a> at this time, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_transit" title="Public transit">public transit</a> systems and especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Streetcar" title="Streetcar">streetcars</a> enabled urban expansion as new residential neighborhoods sprung up along transit lines and workers rode to and from work downtown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hart2001_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hart2001-187">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WhittYago1985_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-WhittYago1985-192">[189]</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nSince the mid-twentieth century, cities have relied heavily on <a href="/wiki/Motor_vehicle" title="Motor vehicle">motor vehicle</a> transportation, with major <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_the_car_on_societies" title="Effects of the car on societies">implications</a> for their layout, environment, and aesthetics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borden_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borden-193">[190]</a></sup> (This transformation occurred most dramatically in the US&#8212;where corporate and governmental policies favored automobile transport systems&#8212;and to a lesser extent in Europe.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hart2001_187-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hart2001-187">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WhittYago1985_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-WhittYago1985-192">[189]</a></sup> The rise of personal <a href="/wiki/Car" title="Car">cars</a> accompanied the expansion of urban economic areas into much larger <a href="/wiki/Metropolis" title="Metropolis">metropolises</a>, subsequently creating ubiquitous <a href="/wiki/Traffic" title="Traffic">traffic</a> issues with accompanying construction of new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Highways" title="Highways">highways</a>, wider streets, and alternative <a href="/wiki/Walkway" title="Walkway">walkways</a> for pedestrians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wachsmuth2014_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wachsmuth2014-152">[151]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cairo_(1546775747).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Cairo_%281546775747%29.jpg/220px-Cairo_%281546775747%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Cairo_%281546775747%29.jpg/330px-Cairo_%281546775747%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Cairo_%281546775747%29.jpg/440px-Cairo_%281546775747%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cairo_(1546775747).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>People walk, drive, and cycle through a street in <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>.</div></div></div><p> However, severe traffic jams still occur regularly in cities around the world, as private car ownership and urbanization continue to increase, overwhelming existing urban <a href="/wiki/Street_network" title="Street network">street networks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gwilliam2013_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gwilliam2013-117">[116]</a></sup>\n</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ggnm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="194" data-file-width="259" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Ggnm.jpg/220px-Ggnm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e0/Ggnm.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ggnm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rapid_Metro_Gurgaon" title="Rapid Metro Gurgaon">Rapid metro</a> on the move in <a href="/wiki/Gurgaon" title="Gurgaon">Gurugram</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The urban <a href="/wiki/Public_transport_bus_service" title="Public transport bus service">bus system</a>, the world\'s most common form of <a href="/wiki/Public_transport" title="Public transport">public transport</a>, uses a network of scheduled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bus_route" title="Bus route">routes</a> to move people through the city, alongside cars, on the roads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[194]</a></sup> Economic function itself also became more decentralized as concentration became impractical and employers relocated to more car-friendly locations (including <a href="/wiki/Edge_city" title="Edge city">edge cities</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hart2001_187-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hart2001-187">[184]</a></sup> Some cities have introduced <a href="/wiki/Bus_rapid_transit" title="Bus rapid transit">bus rapid transit</a> systems which include exclusive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bus_lanes" title="Bus lanes">bus lanes</a> and other methods for prioritizing bus traffic over private cars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gwilliam2013_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gwilliam2013-117">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[195]</a></sup> Many big American cities still operate conventional public transit by rail, as exemplified by the ever-popular <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Subway" title="New York City Subway">New York City Subway</a> system. Rapid transit is widely used in Europe and has increased in Latin America and Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gwilliam2013_117-3"><a href="#cite_note-Gwilliam2013-117">[116]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Walking" title="Walking">Walking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cycling" title="Cycling">cycling</a> ("non-motorized transport") enjoy increasing favor (more <a href="/wiki/Pedestrian_zone" title="Pedestrian zone">pedestrian zones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bike_lane" title="Bike lane">bike lanes</a>) in American and Asian urban transportation planning, under the influence of such trends as the <a href="/wiki/Healthy_city" title="Healthy city">Healthy Cities</a> movement, the drive for <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable development</a>, and the idea of a <a href="/wiki/Carfree_city" title="Carfree city">carfree city</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gwilliam2013_117-4"><a href="#cite_note-Gwilliam2013-117">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[197]</a></sup> Techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Road_space_rationing" title="Road space rationing">road space rationing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Road_pricing" title="Road pricing">road use charges</a> have been introduced to limit urban car traffic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gwilliam2013_117-5"><a href="#cite_note-Gwilliam2013-117">[116]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Housing">Housing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Housing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Housing" title="Housing">Housing</a> of residents presents one of the major challenges every city must face. Adequate housing entails not only physical <a href="/wiki/Shelter_(building)" title="Shelter (building)">shelters</a> but also the physical systems necessary to sustain life and economic activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[198]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Owner-occupancy" title="Owner-occupancy">Home ownership</a> represents status and a modicum of economic security, compared to <a href="/wiki/Renting" title="Renting">renting</a> which may consume much of the income of low-wage urban workers. <a href="/wiki/Homelessness" title="Homelessness">Homelessness</a>, or lack of housing, is a challenged currently faced by millions of people in countries rich and poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[199]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trash_in_Paramaribo.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Trash_in_Paramaribo.JPG/220px-Trash_in_Paramaribo.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Trash_in_Paramaribo.JPG/330px-Trash_in_Paramaribo.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Trash_in_Paramaribo.JPG/440px-Trash_in_Paramaribo.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trash_in_Paramaribo.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This urban scene in <a href="/wiki/Paramaribo" title="Paramaribo">Paramaribo</a> features a few plants growing amidst <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solid_waste" title="Solid waste">solid waste</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rubble" title="Rubble">rubble</a> behind some <a href="/wiki/House" title="House">houses</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Urban_ecology" title="Urban ecology">Urban ecology</a></div>\n<p>Urban <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystems" title="Ecosystems">ecosystems</a>, influenced as they are by the density of human buildings and activities differ considerably from those of their rural surroundings. Anthropogenic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buildings" title="Buildings">buildings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Waste" title="Waste">waste</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">cultivation</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gardens" title="Gardens">gardens</a>, create physical and chemical environments which have no equivalents in <a href="/wiki/Wilderness" title="Wilderness">wilderness</a>, in some cases enabling exceptional <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>. They provide homes not only for immigrant humans but also for <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">immigrant plants</a>, bringing about interactions between species which never previously encountered each other. They introduce frequent <a href="/wiki/Disturbance_(ecology)" title="Disturbance (ecology)">disturbances</a> (construction, walking) to plant and animal <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a>, creating opportunities for <a href="/wiki/Secondary_succession" title="Secondary succession">recolonization</a> and thus favoring <a href="/wiki/Ecological_succession" title="Ecological succession">young ecosystems</a> with <a href="/wiki/R/K_selection_theory" title="R/K selection theory">r-selected species</a> dominant. On the whole, urban ecosystems are less complex and productive than others, due to the diminished absolute amount of biological interactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PickettEtAl2008_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-PickettEtAl2008-205">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[203]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Typical urban <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">fauna</a> include <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a>), <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mouse" title="Mouse">mice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rat" title="Rat">rats</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dogs</a> (<a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domesticated</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feral" title="Feral">feral</a>). Large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">predators</a> are scarce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PickettEtAl2008_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-PickettEtAl2008-205">[202]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Urban_heat_island_(Celsius).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1112" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Urban_heat_island_%28Celsius%29.png/220px-Urban_heat_island_%28Celsius%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Urban_heat_island_%28Celsius%29.png/330px-Urban_heat_island_%28Celsius%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Urban_heat_island_%28Celsius%29.png/440px-Urban_heat_island_%28Celsius%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Urban_heat_island_(Celsius).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Profile of an <a href="/wiki/Urban_heat_island" title="Urban heat island">urban heat island</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Cities generate considerable <a href="/wiki/Ecological_footprint" title="Ecological footprint">ecological footprints</a>, locally and at longer distances, due to concentrated populations and technological activities. From one perspective, cities are not ecologically <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sustainable" title="Sustainable">sustainable</a> due to their resource needs. From another, proper management may be able to ameliorate a city\'s ill effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[205]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">Air pollution</a> arises from various forms of combustion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[206]</a></sup> including fireplaces, wood or coal-burning stoves, other heating systems,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[207]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engines</a>. Industrialized cities, and today third-world megacities, are notorious for veils of <a href="/wiki/Smog" title="Smog">smog</a> (industrial <a href="/wiki/Haze" title="Haze">haze</a>) which envelop them, posing a chronic threat to the health of their millions of inhabitants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adey2013_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adey2013-211">[208]</a></sup> Urban soil contains higher concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">heavy metals</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>) and has lower <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> than soil in comparable wilderness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PickettEtAl2008_205-2"><a href="#cite_note-PickettEtAl2008-205">[202]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Modern cities are known for creating their own <a href="/wiki/Microclimate" title="Microclimate">microclimates</a>, due to <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">asphalt</a>, and other artificial surfaces, which heat up in <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> and channel <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainwater" title="Rainwater">rainwater</a> into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Storm_sewer" title="Storm sewer">underground ducts</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_New_York_City#Climate" title="Geography of New York City">temperature in New York City</a> exceeds <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_New_York" title="Climate of New York">nearby rural temperatures</a> by an average of 2&#8211;3&#160;&#176;C and at times 5&#8211;10&#160;&#176;C differences have been recorded. This effect varies nonlinearly with population changes (independently of the city\'s physical size).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PickettEtAl2008_205-3"><a href="#cite_note-PickettEtAl2008-205">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[209]</a></sup> Aerial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Particulate" title="Particulate">particulates</a> increase rainfall by 5&#8211;10%. Thus, urban areas experience unique climates, with earlier flowering and later leaf dropping than in nearby country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PickettEtAl2008_205-4"><a href="#cite_note-PickettEtAl2008-205">[202]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Poor and working-class people face disproportionate exposure to environmental risks (known as <a href="/wiki/Environmental_racism" title="Environmental racism">environmental racism</a> when intersecting also with racial segregation). For example, within the urban microclimate, less-vegetated poor neighborhoods bear more of the heat (but have fewer means of coping with it).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[210]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_city_system">World city system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: World city system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>As the world becomes more closely linked through economics, politics, technology, and culture (a process called <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a>), cities have come to play a leading role in transnational affairs, exceeding the limitations of <a href="/wiki/International_relations" title="International relations">international relations</a> conducted by national governments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HN2017_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-HN2017-215">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta2015globalization_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta2015globalization-216">[213]</a></sup> This phenomenon, resurgent today, can be traced back to the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>, and the Greek city-states, through the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a> and other alliances of cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CalderFreytas2009_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-CalderFreytas2009-141">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[215]</a></sup> Today the <a href="/wiki/Information_economy" title="Information economy">information economy</a> based on high-speed <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">internet</a> infrastructure enables instantaneous <a href="/wiki/Telecommunication" title="Telecommunication">telecommunication</a> around the world, effectively eliminating the distance between cities for the purposes of <a href="/wiki/Stock_market" title="Stock market">stock markets</a> and other high-level elements of the world economy, as well as personal communications and <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">mass media</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[216]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_city">Global city</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Global city">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:London_Stock_Exchange_(13056321704).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1424" data-file-width="2136" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/London_Stock_Exchange_%2813056321704%29.jpg/220px-London_Stock_Exchange_%2813056321704%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/London_Stock_Exchange_%2813056321704%29.jpg/330px-London_Stock_Exchange_%2813056321704%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/London_Stock_Exchange_%2813056321704%29.jpg/440px-London_Stock_Exchange_%2813056321704%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:London_Stock_Exchange_(13056321704).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Stock_exchange" title="Stock exchange">Stock exchanges</a>, characteristic features of the top global cities, are interconnected hubs for capital. Here, a delegation from Australia is shown visiting the <a href="/wiki/London_Stock_Exchange" title="London Stock Exchange">London Stock Exchange</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Global_city" title="Global city">global city</a>, also known as a world city, is a prominent centre of trade, banking, finance, innovation, and markets. <a href="/wiki/Saskia_Sassen" title="Saskia Sassen">Saskia Sassen</a> used the term "global city" in her 1991 work, <i>The Global City: New York, London, Tokyo</i> to refer to a city\'s <a href="/wiki/Power_(social_and_political)" title="Power (social and political)">power</a>, status, and cosmopolitanism, rather than to its size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[217]</a></sup>  Following this view of cities, it is possible to <a href="/wiki/Global_city#GaWC_study" title="Global city">rank the world\'s cities hierarchically</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ranking_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-ranking-221">[218]</a></sup> Global cities form the capstone of the global hierarchy, exerting <a href="/wiki/Command_and_control" title="Command and control">command and control</a> through their economic and political influence. Global cities may have reached their status due to early transition to <a href="/wiki/Post-industrial_society" title="Post-industrial society">post-industrialism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[219]</a></sup> or through inertia which has enabled them to maintain their dominance from the industrial era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[220]</a></sup> This type of ranking exemplifies an emerging <a href="/wiki/Discourse" title="Discourse">discourse</a> in which cities, considered variations on the same ideal type, <i>must</i> compete with each other globally to achieve prosperity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward2008_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ward2008-159">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wachsmuth2014_152-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wachsmuth2014-152">[151]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Critics of the notion point to the different realms of power and interchange. The term "global city" is heavily influenced by economic factors and, thus, may not account for places that are otherwise significant. <a href="/wiki/Paul_James_(academic)" title="Paul James (academic)">Paul James</a>, for example argues that the term is "reductive and skewed" in its focus on financial systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[221]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">Multinational corporations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bank" title="Bank">banks</a> make their headquarters in global cities and conduct much of their business within this context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[222]</a></sup> American firms dominate the international markets for <a href="/wiki/Law_firm" title="Law firm">law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a> and maintain branches in the biggest foreign global cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[223]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Global cities feature concentrations of extremely wealthy and extremely poor people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[224]</a></sup> Their economies are lubricated by their capacity (limited by the national government\'s immigration policy, which functionally defines the supply side of the labor market) to recruit low- and high-skilled immigrant workers from poorer areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[227]</a></sup> More and more cities today draw on this globally available labor force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[228]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:auto; max-width:1808px;">\n<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:10_mile_panorama_of_NYC,_Feb.,_2018.jpg" title="Modern global cities, like New York City, often include large central business districts (CBDs) that serve as hubs for economic activity. A panorama of <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>, the world\'s largest CBD, is shown from February 2018.<div class="hlist">\n<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Riverside_Church"
